<div style="position: absolute; left: 0px; top: 95px; width: 100px; height: 40px;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Kit_socks_long.svg/100px-Kit_socks_long.svg.png" decoding="async" width="100" height="40" style="vertical-align: top" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Kit_socks_long.svg/150px-Kit_socks_long.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Kit_socks_long.svg/200px-Kit_socks_long.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="100" data-file-height="40" /></div>

<p><b>Derby County Football Club</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/ɑːr/: &#39;ar&#39; in &#39;far&#39;">ɑːr</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is a professional <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">association football</a> club based in <a href="/wiki/Derby" title="Derby">Derby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire" title="Derbyshire">Derbyshire</a>, England. The club competes in the <a href="/wiki/EFL_Championship" title="EFL Championship">EFL Championship</a>, the second tier of English football, and has played its home matches at <a href="/wiki/Pride_Park_Stadium" title="Pride Park Stadium">Pride Park Stadium</a> since 1997.
</p><p>Notable for being one of the 12 founder members of the <a href="/wiki/English_Football_League" title="English Football League">Football League</a> in 1888, Derby County is one of only 10 clubs to have competed in every season of the <a href="/wiki/English_football_league_system" title="English football league system">English football league system</a> and, in 2009, was ranked 137th in the top 200 European football teams of the 20th century by the <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Football_History_%26_Statistics" title="International Federation of Football History &amp; Statistics">International Federation of Football History &amp; Statistics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Europe_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europe-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The club was founded in 1884 by William Morley, as an offshoot of <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire_County_Cricket_Club" title="Derbyshire County Cricket Club">Derbyshire County Cricket Club</a>; it has spent all but four seasons in the top two divisions of English football. Its competitive peak came in the 1970s when it twice won the <a href="/wiki/Football_League_First_Division" title="Football League First Division">First Division</a> and competed in major European competitions on <a href="/wiki/Derby_County_F.C._in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Derby County F.C. in Europe">four separate occasions</a>, reaching the <a href="/wiki/European_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="European Cup">European Cup</a> semi-finals as well as winning several minor trophies. Additionally, the club was a strong force in the interwar years, winning the <a href="/wiki/1945%E2%80%9346_FA_Cup" title="1945–46 FA Cup">1945–46 FA Cup</a>.
</p><p>The club's home colours have been black and white since the 1890s. The team gets its nickname, <i>The Rams</i>, to show tribute to its links with the First Regiment of Derby Militia, which took a <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">ram</a> as its mascot. Additionally adopting the song "<a href="/wiki/The_Derby_Ram_(song)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Derby Ram (song)">The Derby Ram</a>" as its regimental song.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Derby_Ram_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Derby_Ram-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Derby_County_F.C." title="History of Derby County F.C.">History of Derby County F.C.</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Derby_County_F.C._seasons" title="List of Derby County F.C. seasons">List of Derby County F.C. seasons</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DerbyCountyFC_League_Performance.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/DerbyCountyFC_League_Performance.svg/300px-DerbyCountyFC_League_Performance.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/DerbyCountyFC_League_Performance.svg/450px-DerbyCountyFC_League_Performance.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/DerbyCountyFC_League_Performance.svg/600px-DerbyCountyFC_League_Performance.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DerbyCountyFC_League_Performance.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chart of table positions of Derby County in the Football League</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginning_and_early_success">Beginning and early success</span></h3>
<p>Derby County F.C. was formed in 1884 as an offshoot of <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire_County_Cricket_Club" title="Derbyshire County Cricket Club">Derbyshire County Cricket Club</a> in an attempt to give players and supporters a winter interest as well as secure the cricket club extra revenue. The original intention was to name the club "Derbyshire County F.C." to highlight the link, though the Derbyshire FA, formed in 1883, objected on the grounds it was too long and therefore would not have been understood by the fans who may mistake it for a Derbyshire FA team. Playing their home matches at the cricket club's <a href="/wiki/Racecourse_Ground,_Derby" class="mw-redirect" title="Racecourse Ground, Derby">Racecourse Ground</a>, 1884–85 saw the club undertake an extensive programme of friendly matches, the first of which was a 6–0 defeat to Great Lever on 13 September 1884. The club's first competitive match came in the <a href="/wiki/1884%E2%80%9385_FA_Cup" title="1884–85 FA Cup">1885 FA Cup</a>, where they lost 7–0 at home to <a href="/wiki/Walsall_Town_F.C." class="mw-redirect" title="Walsall Town F.C.">Walsall Town</a>.
</p><p>Arguably the most important match in the club's history came in the <a href="/wiki/1885%E2%80%9386_FA_Cup" title="1885–86 FA Cup">following season</a>'s FA Cup, when a 2–0 victory over <a href="/wiki/Aston_Villa_F.C." title="Aston Villa F.C.">Aston Villa</a>, already an emerging force in English football, helped establish Derby County on the English football map, helping the club to attract better opposition for friendlies and, in 1888, an invitation into the inaugural <a href="/wiki/The_Football_League" class="mw-redirect" title="The Football League">Football League</a>. The opening day of the first ever league season was 8 September 1888, when Derby came from 3–0 down away to <a href="/wiki/Bolton_Wanderers_F.C." title="Bolton Wanderers F.C.">Bolton Wanderers</a> to win 6–3, though the club ultimately finished 10th out of 12 teams. In 1891, they absorbed another Derby club, <a href="/wiki/Derby_Midland_F.C." title="Derby Midland F.C.">Derby Midland</a>, which had been a member of the <a href="/wiki/Midland_Football_League_(1889)" title="Midland Football League (1889)">Midland League</a>, leaving them as Derby's sole professional football club. <a href="/wiki/Steve_Bloomer" title="Steve Bloomer">Steve Bloomer</a>, generally considered to be Derby County's best-ever player, joined the club in 1892. In 1895, the club moved to a new stadium, the <a href="/wiki/Baseball_Ground" title="Baseball Ground">Baseball Ground</a> (so called because it was previously used for baseball), which became their home for the next 102 years. It was then that the club adopted their now traditional home colours of black and white.
</p><p>Although Derby were inconsistent in the league, they finished as runners-up to Aston Villa in <a href="/wiki/1895%E2%80%9396_Football_League" title="1895–96 Football League">1896</a>, as well as achieved a number of third-place finishes. They were a strong force in the FA Cup, appearing in three finals in six years around the turn of the 20th century, though lost all three, in 1898 (3–1 to <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Forest_F.C." title="Nottingham Forest F.C.">Nottingham Forest</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-1897FACup_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1897FACup-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> 1899 (4–1 to <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_United_F.C." title="Sheffield United F.C.">Sheffield United</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-1899FACup_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1899FACup-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and 1903 (6–0 to <a href="/wiki/Bury_F.C." title="Bury F.C.">Bury</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-1903FACup_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1903FACup-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Edwardian_and_interwar_era">Edwardian and interwar era</span></h3>
<p>In 1906, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Bloomer" title="Steve Bloomer">Steve Bloomer</a> was sold to <a href="/wiki/Middlesbrough_F.C." title="Middlesbrough F.C.">Middlesbrough</a> due to financial constraints, and Derby subsequently suffered its first ever relegation the following <a href="/wiki/1906%E2%80%9307_Football_League" title="1906–07 Football League">season</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-historyofdcfc_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historyofdcfc-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> but under <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Methven" title="Jimmy Methven">Jimmy Methven</a>'s management, they re-signed Bloomer and regained their <a href="/wiki/Football_League_First_Division" title="Football League First Division">First Division</a> place in 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-historyofdcfc_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historyofdcfc-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In 1914, they were again relegated, but instantly won the Second Division to earn promotion,<sup id="cite_ref-historyofdcfc_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historyofdcfc-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> though World War I meant they had to wait until 1919 to play First Division football again. After two seasons, they were relegated yet again in 1921. However, the appointment of <a href="/wiki/George_Jobey" title="George Jobey">George Jobey</a> in 1925 kick-started a successful period for the <i>Rams</i> and, after promotion in 1926,<sup id="cite_ref-historyofdcfc_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historyofdcfc-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> the club became a formidable force, with high finishes from the late 1920s and all through the 1930s,<sup id="cite_ref-historyofdcfc_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historyofdcfc-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> including finishing as runners-up twice.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brian_Clough_and_Peter_Taylor_Statue_Derby.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Brian_Clough_and_Peter_Taylor_Statue_Derby.jpg/200px-Brian_Clough_and_Peter_Taylor_Statue_Derby.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Brian_Clough_and_Peter_Taylor_Statue_Derby.jpg/300px-Brian_Clough_and_Peter_Taylor_Statue_Derby.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Brian_Clough_and_Peter_Taylor_Statue_Derby.jpg/400px-Brian_Clough_and_Peter_Taylor_Statue_Derby.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3872" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brian_Clough_and_Peter_Taylor_Statue_Derby.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Brian Clough and Peter Taylor statue</div></div></div>
<p>Derby were one of several clubs to close down after the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> but restarted in the early 1940s, in part due to the persistence of Jack Nicholas and Jack Webb. Aided by the recruitment of <a href="/wiki/Raich_Carter" title="Raich Carter">Raich Carter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Doherty_(footballer)" title="Peter Doherty (footballer)">Peter Doherty</a>, who had both been stationed in <a href="/wiki/Loughborough" title="Loughborough">Loughborough</a> during the war, Derby were one step ahead of the opposition when competitive football resumed with the <a href="/wiki/1945%E2%80%9346_FA_Cup" title="1945–46 FA Cup">1946 FA Cup</a> and won their first major trophy with a 4–1 victory over <a href="/wiki/Charlton_Athletic_F.C." title="Charlton Athletic F.C.">Charlton Athletic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FACupWinners_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FACupWinners-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_success_and_decline">Post-war success and decline</span></h3>
<p>The league restarted the following season after a break due to World War II and, under the management of Stuart McMillan, as well as twice breaking the British transfer record to sign <a href="/wiki/Billy_Steel" title="Billy Steel">Billy Steel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Morris_(footballer)" title="Johnny Morris (footballer)">Johnny Morris</a> to replace Carter and Doherty, finished fourth and third in the 1948 and 1949 seasons respectively, before a steady decline set in and the club was relegated in 1953, after nearly 30 years in the top flight, and again in 1955 to drop to the third tier of English football for the first time in their history.<sup id="cite_ref-historyofdcfc_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historyofdcfc-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Harry_Storer,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Harry Storer, Jr.">Harry Storer</a> led Derby back into the second tier at the second attempt in <a href="/wiki/1956%E2%80%9357_in_English_football" title="1956–57 in English football">1957</a>, though the club progressed no further over the next decade under either Storer or his successor, former Derby player <a href="/wiki/Tim_Ward_(footballer)" title="Tim Ward (footballer)">Tim Ward</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brian_Clough_era">Brian Clough era</span></h3>
<p>In 1967, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Clough" title="Brian Clough">Brian Clough</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Taylor_(footballer,_born_1928)" title="Peter Taylor (footballer, born 1928)">Peter Taylor</a> took over and led them to their greatest glory. Having clinched the influential signing of <a href="/wiki/Dave_Mackay" title="Dave Mackay">Dave Mackay</a>, Derby were promoted to the First Division in 1969, finished fourth in 1970,<sup id="cite_ref-historyofdcfc_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historyofdcfc-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> were banned from competing in Europe due to financial irregularities in 1971 and won their first ever Football League Championship in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-historyofdcfc_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historyofdcfc-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Though Derby did not retain their title the following season, they did reach the semi-finals of the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">European Cup</a>, where they lost to <a href="/wiki/Juventus_F.C." title="Juventus F.C.">Juventus</a>. Clough's frequent outspoken comments against football's establishment eventually led to him falling out with the board of directors at the club, and Clough and Taylor left in October 1973. Such was their impact on the club that, 37 years later, a 9&#160;ft (2.75 metres) bronze statue of the pair was erected outside <a href="/wiki/Pride_Park_Stadium" title="Pride Park Stadium">Pride Park</a> in commemoration of their legacy.<sup id="cite_ref-cloughtaylormonument_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cloughtaylormonument-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Success_and_decline_after_Clough">Success and decline after Clough</span></h3><p>
Despite the departure of Clough and Taylor, Derby's League success was repeated in the <a href="/wiki/1974%E2%80%9375_in_English_football" title="1974–75 in English football">1974–75 season</a> when they won the title under successor Dave Mackay, who had been a player under Clough when they had returned to the top flight in 1969. However, Derby's form declined towards the end of the 1970s and they went down to the Second Division in 1980 after a string of managers, including former <a href="/wiki/Manchester_United_F.C." title="Manchester United F.C.">Manchester United</a> boss <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Docherty" title="Tommy Docherty">Tommy Docherty</a>, unsettled the club trying desperately to maintain its place at the top of the First Division. Though they challenged well in their first season, Derby were soon hit hard by rising debts, falling attendances and dismal performances. Peter Taylor returned to the Baseball Ground as manager in early 1983 and kept Derby up that season, but he retired a year later just before Derby's relegation to the <a href="/wiki/Football_League_Third_Division" title="Football League Third Division">Third Division</a> for only the second time in their history. However, Derby did manage to avoid going out of business, and they were soon under the ownership of wealthy businessman <a href="/wiki/Robert_Maxwell" title="Robert Maxwell">Robert Maxwell</a>. </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Derby_Former_Baseball_Ground_Commemoration_by_Denis_O%27Connor.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Derby_Former_Baseball_Ground_Commemoration_by_Denis_O%27Connor.JPG/200px-Derby_Former_Baseball_Ground_Commemoration_by_Denis_O%27Connor.JPG" decoding="async" width="200" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Derby_Former_Baseball_Ground_Commemoration_by_Denis_O%27Connor.JPG/300px-Derby_Former_Baseball_Ground_Commemoration_by_Denis_O%27Connor.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Derby_Former_Baseball_Ground_Commemoration_by_Denis_O%27Connor.JPG/400px-Derby_Former_Baseball_Ground_Commemoration_by_Denis_O%27Connor.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2063" data-file-height="2882" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Derby_Former_Baseball_Ground_Commemoration_by_Denis_O%27Connor.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Derby County's former Baseball Ground commemoration by Denis O'Connor</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s_revival_and_promotion_to_Premier_League">1980s revival and promotion to Premier League</span></h3>
<p>After relegation to the Third Division in May 1984, the club appointed <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Cox_(footballer)" title="Arthur Cox (footballer)">Arthur Cox</a> as manager. Although they missed out on promotion in Cox's first season as manager, they then won back-to-back promotions and were back in the First Division for the <a href="/wiki/1987%E2%80%9388_in_English_football" title="1987–88 in English football">1987–88 season</a>, with attendances also rising as the club's on-the-field fortunes and financial position improved. The financial backing of new chairman Robert Maxwell saw stars such as <a href="/wiki/Peter_Shilton" title="Peter Shilton">Peter Shilton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Wright_(footballer,_born_1963)" title="Mark Wright (footballer, born 1963)">Mark Wright</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dean_Saunders" title="Dean Saunders">Dean Saunders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Hebberd" title="Trevor Hebberd">Trevor Hebberd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ted_McMinn" title="Ted McMinn">Ted McMinn</a> brought to the club and they finished fifth in the <a href="/wiki/1988%E2%80%9389_in_English_football" title="1988–89 in English football">1988–89 season</a>.
</p><p>A lack of any further investment from Maxwell quickly led to a decline, culminating in relegation back to the Second Division in 1991. At this time, local newspaper businessman <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Pickering" title="Lionel Pickering">Lionel Pickering</a> became the majority shareholder of the club, taking control just before Maxwell's death in November 1991. In 1992, Derby County paid £2.5&#160;million for <a href="/wiki/Notts_County_F.C." title="Notts County F.C.">Notts County</a> central defender <a href="/wiki/Craig_Short" title="Craig Short">Craig Short</a>, at the time – and for five years afterwards – the most expensive player to be signed by a club outside the top flight, and indeed one of the highest fees paid by any English club for a player at the time. Other expensive signings included strikers <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Johnson_(footballer,_born_1971)" title="Tommy Johnson (footballer, born 1971)">Tommy Johnson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marco_Gabbiadini" title="Marco Gabbiadini">Marco Gabbiadini</a>. Cox's resignation as manager in October 1993 saw the appointment of legendary former player <a href="/wiki/Roy_McFarland" title="Roy McFarland">Roy McFarland</a> as manager. Derby reached the final of the Division One playoffs that season, but were beaten by local rivals <a href="/wiki/Leicester_City_F.C." title="Leicester City F.C.">Leicester City</a>. McFarland was sacked a year later after Derby missed out on the playoffs, and his replacement was <a href="/wiki/Jim_Smith_(footballer,_born_1940)" title="Jim Smith (footballer, born 1940)">Jim Smith</a> – a manager whose track record at his previous clubs included four promotions and an FA Cup semi-final appearance.
</p><p>Although the <a href="/wiki/1995%E2%80%9396_in_English_football" title="1995–96 in English football">1995–96 season</a> started slowly, the signing of sweeper <a href="/wiki/Igor_%C5%A0timac" title="Igor Štimac">Igor Štimac</a> in the early autumn proved pivotal. Smith guided the <i>Rams</i> to a second-place finish and the <a href="/wiki/Premier_League" title="Premier League">Premier League</a>, now the top flight of English football. During that season, it was announced Derby would be leaving the Baseball Ground after more than 100 years to move into a new all-seater stadium, following earlier plans to develop the Baseball Ground as a 26,000-seat stadium.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After finishing in 12th place in their first season back into the top flight, the club left the Baseball Ground, its home of 102 years, to move into the new 33,597-seat <a href="/wiki/Pride_Park_Stadium" title="Pride Park Stadium">Pride Park Stadium</a> for the <a href="/wiki/1997%E2%80%9398_in_English_football" title="1997–98 in English football">1997–98 season</a>. The Baseball Ground was demolished six years later and a memorial was eventually erected in memory of its role in Derby city history.<sup id="cite_ref-BBGMemorial_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBGMemorial-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relegation_from_top_flight_and_financial_crisis">Relegation from top flight and financial crisis</span></h3>
<p>The club settled well into its new home as it recorded back-to-back top 10 finishes for the first time since their 1970s peak, before a sudden decline at the turn of the millennium saw three years of struggle. Smith resigned to be replaced by former players <a href="/wiki/Colin_Todd" title="Colin Todd">Colin Todd</a>, who lasted just three months, and <a href="/wiki/John_Gregory_(footballer)" title="John Gregory (footballer)">John Gregory</a> before the <i>Rams</i> were relegated after a six-year stay in the top flight, in 2002.
</p><p>Derby County's relegation saw the club enter a serious financial crisis, which forced them to sell many key players. Gregory was later suspended from his managerial duties over alleged misconduct and former <a href="/wiki/Ipswich_Town_F.C." title="Ipswich Town F.C.">Ipswich Town</a> boss <a href="/wiki/George_Burley" title="George Burley">George Burley</a> was brought in. The club was put into <a href="/wiki/Receivership" title="Receivership">receivership</a> then sold in October 2003 for £3 to a group led by <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Keith" title="Jeremy Keith">Jeremy Keith</a>. After finishing 20th in the <a href="/wiki/2003%E2%80%9304_in_English_football" title="2003–04 in English football">2003–04 season</a>, a dramatic improvement in the <a href="/wiki/2004%E2%80%9305_in_English_football" title="2004–05 in English football">2004–05 season</a> saw Derby finish fourth in the <a href="/wiki/Football_League_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="Football League Championship">Championship</a>, qualifying for a promotion play-off spot, though they lost in the semi-finals to <a href="/wiki/Preston_North_End_F.C." title="Preston North End F.C.">Preston North End</a>. Soon afterwards, Burley resigned citing differences between himself and the board. He was replaced by Bolton Wanderers first team coach <a href="/wiki/Phil_Brown_(footballer,_born_1959)" title="Phil Brown (footballer, born 1959)">Phil Brown</a>. In January 2006, Brown was sacked after a poor run of results. Terry Westley, the academy coach at the time, took over first-team duties until the end of the season and saved Derby from relegation.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_the_Premier_League_and_straight_back_to_the_Championship">Return to the Premier League and straight back to the Championship</span></h3>
<p>In April 2006, a consortium of local businessmen led by former vice-chairman <a href="/wiki/Peter_Gadsby" title="Peter Gadsby">Peter Gadsby</a> purchased the club, reducing its debt and returning Pride Park Stadium to the club's ownership in the process. In June 2006, former Preston North End boss <a href="/wiki/Billy_Davies" title="Billy Davies">Billy Davies</a> was appointed Derby County's new permanent manager. In his first season, Davies took Derby to the Championship <a href="/wiki/Play-off" class="mw-redirect" title="Play-off">play-offs</a>, where they beat <a href="/wiki/Southampton_F.C." title="Southampton F.C.">Southampton</a> on penalties in the semi-finals before defeating <a href="/wiki/West_Bromwich_Albion_F.C." title="West Bromwich Albion F.C.">West Bromwich Albion</a> 1–0 with a second-half <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Pearson" title="Stephen Pearson">Stephen Pearson</a> goal at the new <a href="/wiki/Wembley_Stadium" title="Wembley Stadium">Wembley Stadium</a> to secure a return to the Premier League and the associated £60&#160;million windfall.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After failing to win any of their opening five matches of the season (one draw and four consecutive defeats), Derby scored their first victory with a 1-0 win over Newcastle, where <a href="/wiki/Kenny_Miller" title="Kenny Miller">Kenny Miller</a> scored the only goal of the game.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In October 2007, Peter Gadsby stepped down as chairman to be replaced by former <a href="/wiki/Hull_City_A.F.C." title="Hull City A.F.C.">Hull City</a> owner <a href="/wiki/Adam_Pearson_(sports_executive)" title="Adam Pearson (sports executive)">Adam Pearson</a>, who immediately began searching for investment from overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> After a poor start to the season, manager Billy Davies left by mutual consent in November.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> He was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Jewell" title="Paul Jewell">Paul Jewell</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> who failed to save the club as Derby suffered the Premier League's earliest ever relegation, in March,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> recorded the Premier League's lowest-ever points total,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> and equalled <a href="/wiki/Loughborough_F.C." title="Loughborough F.C.">Loughborough</a>'s 108-year Football League record of going through an entire season with only one win, which occurred during the 1899–1900 season when Loughborough finished bottom of the <a href="/wiki/1899%E2%80%931900_Football_League#Second_Division" title="1899–1900 Football League">Second Division</a>. In January 2008, Derby was taken over by an international investment group led by General Sports and Entertainment, with Pearson remaining as <i>de facto</i> chairman.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Derby's match at home to Sheffield United on 13 September 2008 generated much media coverage as it was approaching a year since Derby's last league win, a run which saw the club break the English league record for most matches without a win. Just four days short of the anniversary of the 1–0 victory over <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_United_F.C." title="Newcastle United F.C.">Newcastle United</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rob_Hulse" title="Rob Hulse">Rob Hulse</a> scored against his former club as Derby ran out 2–1 winners, earning Paul Jewell his first league win as Derby boss at his 27th attempt. Despite taking the club to the League Cup semi-final, the club's first major cup semi-final since 1976, where Derby lost 4–3 to Manchester United over two legs, Jewell resigned as manager in December 2008 after a run of just two wins in 11 matches.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> He was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Clough" title="Nigel Clough">Nigel Clough</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> son of former manager Brian Clough. Nigel Clough led the club to 18th place and safety. After two seasons that saw the club finish in the bottom half of the table, Derby finished the two subsequent seasons in 12th and 10th place. Clough was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Steve_McClaren" title="Steve McClaren">Steve McClaren</a> in September 2013; McClaren led the club to a 3rd-place finish in the <a href="/wiki/2013-14_Football_League_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="2013-14 Football League Championship">2013–14 Championship season</a>, but lost the play-off final to <a href="/wiki/Queens_Park_Rangers" class="mw-redirect" title="Queens Park Rangers">Queens Park Rangers</a>. Derby failed to finish in the play-offs the following season, which saw McClaren sacked as local-businessman <a href="/wiki/Mel_Morris_(businessman)" title="Mel Morris (businessman)">Mel Morris</a> assumed control. Morris oversaw a level of spending unprecedented in Derby's history over the following three years, breaking the club's transfer record four times, but also saw an unprecedented managerial turnover as went through six managers between June 2015 and May 2017. In this three-season period, Derby qualified for the play-offs twice, but failed to get past the semi-finals on both occasions.
</p><p>On 31 May 2018, following the departure of manager <a href="/wiki/Gary_Rowett" title="Gary Rowett">Gary Rowett</a> to Stoke, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Lampard" title="Frank Lampard">Frank Lampard</a> was appointed as Derby manager on a three-year contract.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In his first season in charge, he guided the club to a 6th place finish and reached the playoff final at Wembley, but ultimately lost to the hands of <a href="/wiki/Aston_Villa" class="mw-redirect" title="Aston Villa">Aston Villa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> On 4 July 2019, Lampard left Derby to manage former club <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_F.C" class="mw-redirect" title="Chelsea F.C">Chelsea</a>, with his successor, <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Cocu" title="Phillip Cocu">Phillip Cocu</a>, being announced the following day.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Club_crest_and_colours">Club crest and colours</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crest">Crest</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:145px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Derby_County_badge_old.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b7/Derby_County_badge_old.png" decoding="async" width="143" height="154" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="143" data-file-height="154" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption">Derby County's badge from 1946</div></div></div>
<p>Like most old football clubs, Derby County did not initially have any badge displayed on their shirts. Their first badge was introduced in 1924. The badge consisted of a circular shield split into three equally sized sections, representing the club, its fans and the area, all containing items traditionally associated with the city of Derby: a <a href="/wiki/Tudor_rose" title="Tudor rose">Tudor rose</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Crown_(headgear)" title="Crown (headgear)">crown</a> in one section, a <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">buck</a> in a park in the second and a <a href="/wiki/Domestic_sheep" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic sheep">ram</a>'s head in the final section. The badge was worn on the players' shirts for just two seasons before they reverted to plain shirts.
</p><p>By 1934, another badge had been introduced. This time it was a traditionally shaped shield, again with three sections. The buck in the park had been removed and the rose and the crown had been split up and now occupied a section each. The ram's head also remained and was now given the largest section of the shield. The badge never appeared on the players' shirts. The shield was modified in 1946 when the rose and crown were removed and replaced with the letters "DC" (Derby County) and "FC" (Football Club) respectively. The badge, right, was featured on to the player's shirts from its introduction onwards, though the ram's head on its own was used from the late 1960s (the full shield, however, remained the club's official logo).
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Derby_County.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/88/Derby_County.png/150px-Derby_County.png" decoding="async" width="150" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/88/Derby_County.png/225px-Derby_County.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/88/Derby_County.png/300px-Derby_County.png 2x" data-file-width="367" data-file-height="274" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Derby_County.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Derby County's badge from 1997 to 2007</div></div></div>
<p>A new club badge was introduced in 1971, featuring a more modern design that, with modifications, is still in use. The badge was initially consisted of a stylised white ram facing left. The badge was first modified slightly in 1979 to include the text "Derby County FC" under the ram (though the ram remained on its own on away kits). In 1982, the ram turned to face to the right and the text under it was removed. The ram was surrounded by a wreath of laurel and the text "Centenary 1984–1985" was printed underneath for the club's centenary season. The laurel was removed and the text reading "Derby County FC" returned from the next season. In 1993, the ram faced left again and the text was removed once more. From 1995, the ram faced right and was enclosed in a diamond, with a gold banner reading "Derby County FC" underneath and the text "1884" (the year of the club's foundation) underneath that. The design was changed again in 1997 (see left): the ram faced now left and the golden banner now simply read "Derby County"; the diamond and year of formation were removed.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Derby_County_F.C._logo.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/Derby_County_F.C._logo.png/150px-Derby_County_F.C._logo.png" decoding="async" width="150" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/Derby_County_F.C._logo.png/225px-Derby_County_F.C._logo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/01/Derby_County_F.C._logo.png 2x" data-file-width="265" data-file-height="264" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Derby_County_F.C._logo.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Derby County's badge from 2009 to 2013, a version of this badge with gold trim was used from 2007 to 2009.</div></div></div>
<p>A decade later, in 2007, the badge was modified again with the ram still facing left and the text "Est. 1884" now in the middle of a circular frame featuring "Derby County Football Club" in gold lettering, with the colours being modified to the club colours of black and white in 2009 (see top of page). In July 2012, the club announced its intention to show only the iconic ram on future shirts, rather than the full club logo. In July 2013, this traditional ram became the club's full logo again.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colours">Colours</span></h3>
<p>Derby County's original colours (right) were amber, chocolate and blue, though by the 1890s the club had adopted its now traditional colours of black and white, which are still in use today. In the 1970s and 1980s, colours for home matches were white shirts with small blue or red touches (on the club badge or shirt makers insignia), blue shorts and socks that were blue, red, white or a combination of the three.<sup id="cite_ref-DerbyKits_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DerbyKits-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The colours of away kits have varied widely, and although they are usually yellow/gold or blue, the colour for the away kit for the 2008–09 season was fluorescent green.<sup id="cite_ref-AwayKits_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AwayKits-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The club also introduced a surprise third kit in August 2008. Similar in design to the club's away kit of the 1970s, with blue and white stripes and reminiscent of the <a href="/wiki/Argentina_national_football_team" title="Argentina national football team">Argentina national team</a> strip, the style was re-introduced following feedback from fans who said it was one of their favourite kits from the club's past.<sup id="cite_ref-ThirdKit_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ThirdKit-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright" style=""><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px"><div class="thumbimage noresize" style="width:120px;"><div style="width: 100px; margin: 0 auto; padding: 0;">
<div style="position: relative; left: 0px; top: 0px; width: 100px; height: 135px; margin: 0 auto; padding: 0;">
<div style="position: absolute; left: 0px; top: 0px; width: 31px; height: 59px; background-color: #AAD0FF;"></div>

<div style="position: absolute; left: 31px; top: 0px; width: 38px; height: 59px;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Kit_body.svg/38px-Kit_body.svg.png" decoding="async" width="38" height="59" style="vertical-align: top" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Kit_body.svg/57px-Kit_body.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Kit_body.svg/76px-Kit_body.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="38" data-file-height="59" /></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Club_mascot">Club mascot</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rammie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Rammie.jpg/220px-Rammie.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Rammie.jpg/330px-Rammie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Rammie.jpg/440px-Rammie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rammie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Derby County's mascot, Rammie</div></div></div>
<p>Derby's mascot is a ram named Rammie, who also works to maintain the club's links with fans and the East Midlands in general, such as school visits to promote literacy and charity events.<sup id="cite_ref-Rammie_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rammie-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RammiePledge_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RammiePledge-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Rammie originally emerged as a more friendly option to the club's traditional links with the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mercian_Regiment" title="Mercian Regiment">Mercian Regiment</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mercian_Regiment#Mascot" title="Mercian Regiment">particular</a>.
</p><p>Rammie was the first full-time mascot in British football.<sup id="cite_ref-RammieDVD_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RammieDVD-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Rammie's traditional activities include penalty shoot-outs with members of the crowd at half-time, with Rammie as goalkeeper, and warming the crowd up before the match and encouraging the Derby fans during matches. Rammie is a very popular figure amongst Rams fans<sup id="cite_ref-RammieFC_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RammieFC-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rammiedream_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rammiedream-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and, in 2005, released his first DVD, which features the character reading from <a href="/wiki/Aesop%27s_Fables" title="Aesop&#39;s Fables">Aesop's Fables</a> in the Derbyshire countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-RammieDVD_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RammieDVD-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly thereafter, Rammie was joined by a female equivalent, named Eweie. However, Eweie did not last very long at Pride Park, and took a reported "vacation" to the United States. She returned from a 10-year exile on 3 October 2015 at a home match against <a href="/wiki/Brentford_F.C." title="Brentford F.C.">Brentford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stadium">Stadium</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pride_Park.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Pride_Park.jpg/300px-Pride_Park.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Pride_Park.jpg/450px-Pride_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Pride_Park.jpg/600px-Pride_Park.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="369" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pride_Park.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pride Park Stadium has been Derby's home since 1997</div></div></div>
<p>As an offshoot of the cricket club, Derby County's first home stadium was the <a href="/wiki/County_Cricket_Ground,_Derby" title="County Cricket Ground, Derby">County Cricket Ground</a>, also known as the Racecourse Ground, where the club played between 1884 and 1895. Although the ground itself was good enough to hold the first FA Cup final match outside <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Blackburn_Rovers_F.C." title="Blackburn Rovers F.C.">Blackburn Rovers</a> beat West Bromwich Albion 2–0 in the <a href="/wiki/1886_FA_Cup_Final" title="1886 FA Cup Final">1886 FA Cup final replay</a><sup id="cite_ref-1886CupFinal_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1886CupFinal-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and a full <a href="/wiki/England_national_football_team" title="England national football team">England international</a>, disputes over fixture clashed between the football and cricket clubs meant that when the opportunity to play at <a href="/wiki/Francis_Ley" title="Francis Ley">Sir Francis Ley's</a> <a href="/wiki/Baseball_Ground" title="Baseball Ground">Baseball Ground</a> arose, the club accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-BBGArtcicle_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBGArtcicle-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Commonly referred to amongst supporters as "the BBG", the club moved to the Baseball Ground in 1895 and remained there for the next 102 years, despite opportunities to move in the 1920s and 1940s.<sup id="cite_ref-BBGArtcicle_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBGArtcicle-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Derby had already played there, a 1–0 win over <a href="/wiki/Sunderland_A.F.C" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunderland A.F.C">Sunderland</a> during the <a href="/wiki/1891%E2%80%9392_Football_League" title="1891–92 Football League">1891–92 season</a>, as an alternative venue after a fixture clash at the County Ground. At its peak during the late 1960s, the ground could hold around 42,000 – the club's record attendance achieved following the opening of the Ley Stand with a 41,826 crowd watching a 5–0 defeat of <a href="/wiki/Tottenham_Hotspur_F.C." title="Tottenham Hotspur F.C.">Tottenham Hotspur</a> on 20 September 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-BBGArtcicle_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBGArtcicle-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> From this peak, the continued addition of seating saw the capacity drop over the next 15 years to 26,500 in 1985.
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Report" title="Taylor Report">Taylor Report</a> in 1989, and the legal requirement for all seater stadia, the ground's capacity dwindled to just 18,500 by the mid-1990s, not enough for the then ambitious second tier club. Despite initially hoping to rebuild the Baseball Ground to hold 26,000 spectators, and rejecting the offer of two sites elsewhere in Derby, then-chairman <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Pickering" title="Lionel Pickering">Lionel Pickering</a> announced in February 1996 the intention to move to a new, purpose built stadium at the newly regenerated <a href="/wiki/Pride_Park" title="Pride Park">Pride Park</a>, with the last ever first team game at the Baseball Ground being in May 1997, a 1–3 home defeat to <a href="/wiki/Arsenal_F.C." title="Arsenal F.C.">Arsenal</a>, though it continued to host reserve matches until 2003. Derby's new ground, named <a href="/wiki/Pride_Park_Stadium" title="Pride Park Stadium">Pride Park Stadium</a>, was officially opened by the <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth II of the United Kingdom">Queen</a> on 18 July with a friendly against Italian club <a href="/wiki/U.C._Sampdoria" title="U.C. Sampdoria">Sampdoria</a> following on 4 August.
</p><p>Derby hold the unique distinction of being the only club to have had three home grounds host full England internationals. England beat <a href="/wiki/Ireland_national_football_team_(1882%E2%80%931950)" title="Ireland national football team (1882–1950)">Ireland</a> 9–0 at the <a href="/wiki/County_Cricket_Ground,_Derby" title="County Cricket Ground, Derby">Racecourse Ground</a> in 1895, beat Ireland again, 2–1, at the Baseball Ground in 1911 and, most recently, Pride Park hosted England's 4–0 win over <a href="/wiki/Mexico_national_football_team" title="Mexico national football team">Mexico</a> in May 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pride Park was renamed the iPro Stadium on 7 December 2013, as part of a 10-year, £7&#160;million sponsorship deal with global sports drink company iPro. At the beginning of 2017, it reverted to its original name of Pride Park Stadium.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Supporters_and_rivalry">Supporters and rivalry</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Support">Support</span></h3>
<p>Derby is often acknowledged as a "passionate football town" by rival supporters<sup id="cite_ref-FootballGdGuide_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FootballGdGuide-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and the press alike. Tony Francis of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i> noted, "Derby is a passionate football town... Even in Division Two, it's a reasonable bet that crowds at Pride Park would not fall far below 20,000. It's historical, it's geographical, it's in the blood. Some places have it, some don't."<sup id="cite_ref-CoffeeCup_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoffeeCup-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> During the 2007–08 Premier League season, Derby County fans were repeatedly referred to as the best in the country due to their loyalty despite the club's disastrous campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-SpursHome_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SpursHome-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Almost every home match at Pride Park Stadium was sold out by the Derby fans and the club also had a large following away from home. The recognition included them being named fans of the season in much national coverage of the season, winning an award from <a href="/wiki/Nuts_(magazine)" title="Nuts (magazine)"><i>Nuts</i> magazine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-RSGoesNuts_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSGoesNuts-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and being named the most loyal supporters in the country in a 2008 survey by <i>Sky Sports Magazine</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-LoyalSupporters_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LoyalSupporters-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, Derby supporter Nick Webster was voted <a href="/wiki/Football_League_Awards#2013_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="Football League Awards">Championship Fan of the Year</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nickwebster_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nickwebster-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Statistically, the club had the 12th-highest average attendance in the country in the 2007–08 season,<sup id="cite_ref-attendance_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-attendance-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> 2008–09,<sup id="cite_ref-attendance2009_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-attendance2009-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and 2009–10 seasons,<sup id="cite_ref-attendance2010_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-attendance2010-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> despite only having the <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_football_stadia_by_capacity" class="mw-redirect" title="List of English football stadia by capacity">15th-largest club ground</a> and finishing 18th or lower in their respective division. In 2008–09, they were the best supported club in the Championship, with a larger average attendance than nine Premier League clubs, and had the Football Leagues' single largest league match attendance, with 33,079 against <a href="/wiki/Wolverhampton_Wanderers_F.C." title="Wolverhampton Wanderers F.C.">Wolverhampton Wanderers</a> on 13 April 2009.
</p><p>Derby's celebrity supporters include actor <a href="/wiki/Robert_Lindsay_(actor)" title="Robert Lindsay (actor)">Robert Lindsay</a>, <a href="/wiki/One_Direction" title="One Direction">One Direction</a> singer <a href="/wiki/Niall_Horan" title="Niall Horan">Niall Horan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Blur_(band)" title="Blur (band)">Blur</a> guitarist <a href="/wiki/Graham_Coxon" title="Graham Coxon">Graham Coxon</a><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Gaslight_Anthem" title="The Gaslight Anthem">The Gaslight Anthem</a> guitarist <a href="/wiki/Alex_Rosamilia" title="Alex Rosamilia">Alex Rosamilia</a><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> adult film star <a href="/wiki/Keiran_Lee" title="Keiran Lee">Keiran Lee</a><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> and actor <a href="/wiki/Jack_O%27Connell_(actor)" title="Jack O&#39;Connell (actor)">Jack O'Connell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> It has been reported that O'Connell has persuaded other celebrities to support the club, including actors <a href="/wiki/George_Clooney" title="George Clooney">George Clooney</a><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Angelina_Jolie" title="Angelina Jolie">Angelina Jolie</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and model <a href="/wiki/Cara_Delevingne" title="Cara Delevingne">Cara Delevingne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivals">Rivals</span></h3>
<p>Derby's primary rival clubs are <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Forest_F.C." title="Nottingham Forest F.C.">Nottingham Forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leicester_City_F.C." title="Leicester City F.C.">Leicester City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leeds_United_F.C." title="Leeds United F.C.">Leeds United</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-top3rivals_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-top3rivals-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> with Forest, based in <a href="/wiki/Nottingham" title="Nottingham">Nottingham</a>, 14 miles east of Derby, being by far the fiercest rivals; a 2008 survey named the rivalry the 11th-largest in English football, revealing that nine out of 10 fans from both clubs point to the other as their fiercest rival.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Meetings between the side are known as <a href="/wiki/Derby_County_F.C.%E2%80%93Nottingham_Forest_F.C._rivalry" title="Derby County F.C.–Nottingham Forest F.C. rivalry">East Midlands derbies</a> and the winning team is awarded the <a href="/wiki/Brian_Clough_Trophy" title="Brian Clough Trophy">Brian Clough Trophy</a>. The rivalry as a whole largely developed from the 1970s, due to former Derby manager <a href="/wiki/Brian_Clough" title="Brian Clough">Brian Clough</a> taking over at Forest, much to the anger of the Derby fans. In fact, some commentators have described the rivalry to be as much about which club owns Clough's heart as much as the proximity of the clubs geographically.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The rivalry with Leicester City stems largely from geographical location rather than any shared history.<sup id="cite_ref-Rivals_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rivals-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Leeds United are disliked due to ongoing friction from the early 1970s when Derby and Leeds were two of the top English teams and the scarcely concealed hostility between their respective managers, Brian Clough and <a href="/wiki/Don_Revie" title="Don Revie">Don Revie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rivals_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rivals-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The rivalry is documented in the <a href="/wiki/The_Damned_Utd" title="The Damned Utd">novel</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Damned_United" title="The Damned United">film</a> <i>The Damned United</i>. This rivalry is stronger on Derby's side: while Derby consider Leeds their second-biggest rivals, Leeds fans focus more on their dislike of Manchester United and <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_F.C." title="Chelsea F.C.">Chelsea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-top3rivals_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-top3rivals-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Derby_County_F.C._Reserves_and_Academy" title="Derby County F.C. Reserves and Academy">Derby County F.C. Reserves and Academy</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_former_players">Notable former players</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Derby_County_F.C._players" title="List of Derby County F.C. players">List of Derby County F.C. players</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English_Football_Hall_of_Fame_members">English Football Hall of Fame members</span></h3>
<p>Several ex-players/managers associated with Derby County are represented in the <a href="/wiki/English_Football_Hall_of_Fame" title="English Football Hall of Fame">English Football Hall of Fame</a>, which was created in 2002 as a celebration of those who have achieved at the very peak of the English game. To be considered for induction players/managers must be 30 years of age or older and have played/managed for at least five years in England.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>2002 – <span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/England" title="England"><img alt="England" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/23px-Flag_of_England.svg.png" Clough">Brian Clough</a>  <span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland"><img alt="Northern Ireland" <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Football_League_100_Legends">Football League 100 Legends</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Football_League_100_Legends" title="Football League 100 Legends">Football League 100 Legends</a> is a list of "100 legendary football players" produced by The Football League in 1998, to celebrate the 100th season of League football. Eight former Derby players made the list.
</p>
<ul><li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/England" title="England"><img alt="England" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/23px-Flag_of_England.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="14" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/35px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/46px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="480" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/John_Goodall" title="John Goodall">John Goodall</a></li>

<h3><span id="The_Jack_Stamps_Trophy_.28Player_of_the_Year.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Jack_Stamps_Trophy_(Player_of_the_Year)">The Jack Stamps Trophy (Player of the Year)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Derby_County_F.C._Player_of_the_Year" title="Derby County F.C. Player of the Year">Derby County F.C. Player of the Year</a></div>
<p>Derby County's Player of the Season award is voted for by the club's supporters and named in honour of <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Stamps" title="Jackie Stamps">Jackie Stamps</a>, who scored two goals in Derby's sole <a href="/wiki/FA_Cup" title="FA Cup">FA Cup</a> final victory in 1946. It was first introduced in the <a href="/wiki/1968%E2%80%9369_in_English_football" title="1968–69 in English football">1968–69 season</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Managers">Managers</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Derby_County_F.C._managers" title="List of Derby County F.C. managers">List of Derby County F.C. managers</a></div>
<p>Below is a list of all the permanent managers that Derby County have had since the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Harry_Newbould" title="Harry Newbould">Harry Newbould</a> in 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-Managers_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Managers-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In the 16 years prior to Newbould's appointment, the team was selected by club committee, a standard practice by football clubs at the time.

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Club_academy">Club academy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moor_Farm">Moor Farm</span></h3>
<p>Derby County's academy, called Moor Farm, is a purpose-built complex situated near the city suburb of <a href="/wiki/Oakwood,_Derbyshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Oakwood, Derbyshire">Oakwood</a>. It was built in 2003, at a cost of £5&#160;million,<sup id="cite_ref-Academy_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academy-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> to replace the club's previous academy, The Ram-Arena, which was based at Raynesway. It covers 50 acres (200,000&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) and features six full-sized training pitches plus an indoor pitch and includes a gym, restaurant, ProZone room and a laundry.<sup id="cite_ref-Academy_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academy-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> When opening the academy, then-Chairman <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Pickering" title="Lionel Pickering">Lionel Pickering</a> said that the intent was to have "at least eight players from the Academy... in the first-team within three years."<sup id="cite_ref-Academy_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academy-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Although this was not achieved, the academy produced a number of notable players, including England international midfielder <a href="/wiki/Tom_Huddlestone" title="Tom Huddlestone">Tom Huddlestone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales_national_football_team" title="Wales national football team">Wales international</a> defender <a href="/wiki/Lewin_Nyatanga" title="Lewin Nyatanga">Lewin Nyatanga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_national_football_team" title="Northern Ireland national football team">Northern Ireland international</a> goalkeeper <a href="/wiki/Lee_Camp_(footballer)" title="Lee Camp (footballer)">Lee Camp</a>, <a href="/wiki/England_national_under-21_football_team" title="England national under-21 football team">England under-21s</a> players <a href="/wiki/Miles_Addison" title="Miles Addison">Miles Addison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lee_Grant_(footballer,_born_1983)" title="Lee Grant (footballer, born 1983)">Lee Grant</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/England_national_under-19_football_team" title="England national under-19 football team">England under-19</a> player <a href="/wiki/Giles_Barnes" title="Giles Barnes">Giles Barnes</a>.
</p><p>In April 2009, new manager Nigel Clough announced his intention to restructure the academy, appointing former Derby players <a href="/wiki/Darren_Wassall" title="Darren Wassall">Darren Wassall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Forsyth_(footballer)" title="Michael Forsyth (footballer)">Michael Forsyth</a><sup id="cite_ref-wassall_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wassall-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> and Wolverhampton Wanderers Academy director John Perkins to the backroom staff, replacing the departed Phil Cannon, <a href="/wiki/David_Lowe_(footballer)" title="David Lowe (footballer)">David Lowe</a> and Brian Burrows.<sup id="cite_ref-Cannon_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cannon-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Following this, and an increased investment of £1&#160;million per year from the club,<sup id="cite_ref-walsallinterview_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walsallinterview-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> a number of players broke through to the first team squad; ahead of the <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9311_Derby_County_F.C._season#Current_squad" title="2010–11 Derby County F.C. season">2010–11 season</a>, almost a third of the Derby squad were academy graduates, with <a href="/wiki/Mason_Bennett" title="Mason Bennett">Mason Bennett</a> setting the club record for youngest first team appearance when he made his full debut with a start in a defeat at Middlesbrough on 22 October 2011 at the age of 15 years and 99 days old.<sup id="cite_ref-Bennettbreaksrecord_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bennettbreaksrecord-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> This helped strengthen the academy's reputation<sup id="cite_ref-glickmidlandsbeacon_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-glickmidlandsbeacon-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> and reinforced CEO Tom Glick's stated desire to make Moor Farm "the academy of choice in the Midlands."<sup id="cite_ref-glickmidlandsbeacon_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-glickmidlandsbeacon-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In August 2012, Derby's academy became a tier 2 academy under the new controversial <a href="/wiki/Elite_Player_Performance_Plan" title="Elite Player Performance Plan">Elite Player Performance Plan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tier2_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tier2-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> It was awarded Tier 1 status two years later in July 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-tier1_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tier1-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</p>

<ul><li>Champions: <a href="/wiki/1956%E2%80%9357_in_English_football" title="1956–57 in English football">1956–57</a> (North)</li>

<ul><li>Winners: <a href="/wiki/1975%E2%80%9376_in_English_football" title="1975–76 in English football">1975</a></li></ul></li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="125th_anniversary">125th anniversary</span></h2>
<p>As part of the club's 125th Anniversary in 2009, the Derby board took a number of initiatives to celebrate the club's history.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="All-time_XI">All-time XI</span></h3>

</div></div><div style="text-align:center; font-size:smaller">Derby County F.C. All Time First XI</div></div></div>
<p>As part of the club's 125th Anniversary celebrations,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> it was announced that during 2009 each month a vote would be carried out to decide on the club's official All Time XI, starting in February 2009 with the <a href="/wiki/Goalkeeper_(association_football)" title="Goalkeeper (association football)">goalkeeper</a>, with the following eight months offering opportunities for Derby's support to select a team based within a <a href="/wiki/Formation_(association_football)#4–4–2" title="Formation (association football)">4–4–2 formation</a>, with December's vote being reserved for the manager.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Voting closed on the 25th of each month, with the winner being announced in the following few days.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Top_10_Derby_goals">Top 10 Derby goals</span></h3>
<p>On 2 June 2009, the Club announced the supporters choice of the Top 10 Goals in the club's history, with the fans then asked again to choose their favourite from the 10 nominated. The list was obviously biased in favour of more recent goals, largely thanks to the increased coverage modern football enjoys. Three goals featured from the club's 2008/09 campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The winners were announced on 22 June 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Derby_County_in_Europe">Derby County in Europe</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Derby_County_F.C._in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Derby County F.C. in Europe">Derby County F.C. in Europe</a></div>
<p>Derby first competed in Europe when they entered the <a href="/wiki/European_Cup_1972-73" class="mw-redirect" title="European Cup 1972-73">1972–73 European Cup</a> after winning the <a href="/wiki/1971%E2%80%9372_in_English_football#First_Division" title="1971–72 in English football">1971–72 First Division Title</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-DerbyInEurope_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DerbyInEurope-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> reaching the semi-final stages, where they lost 3–1 on aggregate to <a href="/wiki/Juventus_F.C." title="Juventus F.C.">Juventus</a> in controversial circumstances. They had qualified for the <a href="/wiki/Inter-Cities_Fairs_Cup_1970-71" class="mw-redirect" title="Inter-Cities Fairs Cup 1970-71">1970–71 Fairs Cup</a> after finishing the <a href="/wiki/1969%E2%80%9370_in_English_football#First_Division" title="1969–70 in English football">1969–70 First Division</a> in 4th, but were banned from entering the competition for financial irregularities. The 70s was the Derby County's peak in English football and they qualified for Europe in three of the next four seasons, competing in the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="UEFA Cup">UEFA Cup</a> or the <a href="/wiki/European_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="European Cup">European Cup</a> in each of the three seasons between <a href="/wiki/1974%E2%80%9375_in_English_football" title="1974–75 in English football">1974–75</a> and <a href="/wiki/1976%E2%80%9377_in_English_football" title="1976–77 in English football">1976–77</a>.
</p><p>The club then declined rapidly and has not appeared in the top European competitions since, though it finished in 5th in the <a href="/wiki/1988%E2%80%9389_in_English_football" title="1988–89 in English football">1989 First Division</a> which would have guaranteed entry into the <a href="/wiki/1989%E2%80%9390_UEFA_Cup" title="1989–90 UEFA Cup">1989–90 UEFA Cup</a> had English Clubs not been banned from Europe following the <a href="/wiki/Heysel_Stadium_Disaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Heysel Stadium Disaster">Heysel Stadium Disaster</a>.
</p><p>Outside of major competition, the club competed in the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Italian_Cup" title="Anglo-Italian Cup">Anglo-Italian Cup</a> between <a href="/wiki/1992%E2%80%9393_in_English_football" title="1992–93 in English football">1992–93</a> and <a href="/wiki/1994%E2%80%9395_in_English_football" title="1994–95 in English football">1994–95</a>, reaching the final in 1993, losing 3–1 to <a href="/wiki/U.S._Cremonese" title="U.S. Cremonese">Cremonese</a> at <a href="/wiki/Wembley_Stadium" title="Wembley Stadium">Wembley</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Records_and_statistics">Records and statistics</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Derby_County_F.C._records_and_statistics" title="List of Derby County F.C. records and statistics">List of Derby County F.C. records and statistics</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Derby_County_F.C._league_record_by_opponent" title="Derby County F.C. league record by opponent">Derby County F.C. league record by opponent</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kevin_Hector" title="Kevin Hector">Kevin Hector</a> holds the record for Derby County appearances in all competitions, appearing 589 times in two separate spells with the club between 1966 and 1982. He sits ahead of <a href="/wiki/Ron_Webster" title="Ron Webster">Ron Webster</a>, who played 535 times for the club, often in the same team as Hector. Just counting league appearances, Hector is again in the lead with 486 appearances, ahead of Jack Parry, who played 483 times for the club between 1948 and 1967.
</p><p>The club's all-time top goalscorer is <a href="/wiki/Steve_Bloomer" title="Steve Bloomer">Steve Bloomer</a>, often referred to as "Football's First Superstar", who netted 332 goals for the club in two spells between 1892 and 1914. He is over 100 goals ahead of second in the list Kevin Hector, who netted 201 goals for the club. <a href="/wiki/Jack_Bowers" title="Jack Bowers">Jack Bowers</a> holds the club record for most goals in a single season, when he scored 43 goals (35 in the league and a further 8 in the FA Cup), during the <a href="/wiki/1932%E2%80%9333_in_English_football" title="1932–33 in English football">1932–33 season</a>.
</p><p>The club's record attendance is 41,826, for a <a href="/wiki/Football_League_First_Division" title="Football League First Division">First Division</a> match against Tottenham Hotspur at the Baseball Ground on 20 September 1969, which Derby won 5–0. The record is unlikely to be broken in the near future as Derby's current stadium, Pride Park Stadium, has a limit of 33,597 spectators. The record attendance at Pride Park for a competitive Derby County match is 33,378 for a Premier League match against <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_F.C." title="Liverpool F.C.">Liverpool</a> on 18 March 2000. The largest crowd to ever watch a Derby County game is 120,000 when Derby County played <a href="/wiki/Real_Madrid_C.F." class="mw-redirect" title="Real Madrid C.F.">Real Madrid</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Santiago_Bernab%C3%A9u_Stadium" title="Santiago Bernabéu Stadium">Santiago Bernabéu Stadium</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1975%E2%80%9376_European_Cup" title="1975–76 European Cup">1975–76 European Cup</a>.
</p><p>Derby's historically poor <a href="/wiki/Derby_County_F.C._season_2007%E2%80%9308" class="mw-redirect" title="Derby County F.C. season 2007–08">2007–08 Premier League campaign</a> saw the club set and equal several unwanted <a href="/wiki/Football_records_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Football records in England">records in English football</a>. The club equaled Loughborough's all-time league record of just one win in an entire league season. They also equaled or set several Premier League records (1992–present), including fewest home wins in a season (1, joint with Sunderland), fewest away wins in a season (0, joint with five other clubs), and most defeats in a season (29). Records set included fewest points in a season (three points for a win) with 11; fewest goals scored (20), and worst goal difference (−69).<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The club also holds the joint record (with <a href="/wiki/Macclesfield_Town" class="mw-redirect" title="Macclesfield Town">Macclesfield Town</a>) for most consecutive league games without a win, with 36 matches over two seasons between 22 September 2007 and 13 September 2008.
</p><p>Derby also own (alongside <a href="/wiki/Watford_F.C." title="Watford F.C.">Watford</a>), the joint record for most lopsided defeat in an FA Cup final, a 6–0 loss to Bury in <a href="/wiki/1903_FA_Cup_Final" title="1903 FA Cup Final">1903</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Derby_County_Stats_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Derby_County_Stats-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Women.27s_Team"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Women's_Team">Women's Team</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Derby_County_L.F.C." title="Derby County L.F.C.">Derby County L.F.C.</a> – Women's football club associated with Derby County F.C.
</p>
